            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3c3ee9edb5f04ae1801efe7dc02f098d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '13ec1d9c-8016-424c-a07f-cd040ac8fe76'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zhaoyong@henu.edu.cn"><span>zhaoyong@henu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhao/Yong">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d250121874" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Polymer-based sodium metal battery (SMB) has a great development prospect due to its low cost, good safety, and high theoretical energy density. However, sodium (Na) metal electrode suffers low cyclic stability due to the naturally-formed unstable solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Herein, 3D network polymer, poly-bis(3-(2-methylaziridin-1-yl) propanoate (PBMP) is synthesized, for mechanically robust gel polymer electrolyte (GPE), in which the tertiary-amine functional group with low-valence N prefers to react with Na metal to produce a thin and ion-conducive SEI, realizing Na<sup>+</sup> fast and uniform plating/stripping. The assembled Na/GPE/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full battery shows an ultrahigh capacity retention of 97.8% after 2000 cycles under 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, which is the top-level of GPE-SMB. This work provides a new strategy to construct advanced SEI by regulating functional groups of network polymer for GPE-SMBs with high safety.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303791-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303791-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Polymer-based sodium metal battery (SMB) is considered as one of the most promising next-generation energy storage devices because of its high theoretical energy density, low cost, and good safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0001_R_d250121866e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0005_R_d250121866e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, sodium (Na) metal anode always faces the issue of low cyclic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0006_R_d250121866e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The naturally-formed solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on Na metal in gel polymer electrolyte (GPE) always contains large number of organic species with low ionic conductivity, causing the dendrite growth and side reactions. It not only consumes active Na metal to form a thick SEI layer with low ion conductivity, but also results in the low Coulombic efficiency of Na metal electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0007_R_d250121866e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0009_R_d250121866e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Exploring an advanced SEI on Na metal electrodes play an important role to promote the practical use of GPE-SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0010_R_d250121866e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Researchers have devoted great efforts to improve the cyclic stability of Na metal such as constructing in situ SEI with inorganic species through regulating the solvation structure of liquid electrolyte,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0011_R_d250121866e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0013_R_d250121866e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> pretreating Na with metal alloying to decrease the nucleation potential of Na metal and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0014_R_d250121866e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0016_R_d250121866e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Xia et&nbsp;al. used sodium nitrate as an additive to construct a N-rich SEI layer on the surface of Na metal; Zhou et&nbsp;al. used SnF<sub>2</sub> to react with molten Na to obtain a Na<sub>x</sub>Sn alloys for extending cycle life of Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0017_R_d250121866e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although those methods can improve the stability of Na metal, the limited ion conductivity of SEI with high content of organic species seems to be the crucial reason for the low cyclic stability of Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0018_R_d250121866e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another shortage for this technique may be the use of liquid electrolytes with low safety for SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0019_R_d250121866e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0022_R_d250121866e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Constructing GPE with network polymers showed great promise to improve the battery safety, which is also effective to extend the cycle life of Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0023_R_d250121866e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is due to the robustness of GPE facilitates to inhibit the dendrite growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0024_R_d250121866e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Wu et&nbsp;al. introduced ethoxylated trimethylolpropane triacrylate (ETPEA) monomer into liquid carbonate electrolytes for constructing ETPEA-based quasi-solid-state electrolyte, the Na metal plating/stripping behavior becomes more uniform due to the increased mechanical strength of network Polymer-based GPE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0025_R_d250121866e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the SEI layer derived from network polymer or electrolyte additive is still not enough for sustaining the long-term stability of Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0014_R_d250121866e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the advanced strategy to construct GPE with high mechanical strength and the derived SEI with good ionic conductivity is highly potential to realize high performance of GPE-SMBs with good safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0026_R_d250121866e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0029_R_d250121866e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we proposed a new strategy that the 3D network polymer, polybis(3-(2-methylaziridin-1-yl) propanoate (PBMP), with low-valence N element can react with Na metal to produce Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> species rich SEI with high ionic conductivity. The PBMP-based GPE not only has a high ionic conductivity to promote Na<sup>+</sup> fast transport but also has a high mechanical strength to inhibit Na dendrite growth. It enables long cyclic stability for Na metal in the GPE-SMBs even with a high an area capacity of 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Importantly, different from the high valance N element from nitrate salt additives, the N element in PBMP has a low valance and shows the priority to react with Na metal. As a result, the cycling stability of PBMP-based Na/GPE/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full battery shows an ultrahigh capacity retention of 97.8% after 2000 cycles under 5&nbsp;C (2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), which is the top-level of GPE-SMB. It is believed that this work provides a new strategy to construct stable SEI by regulating functional groups of network polymer for building high-performance GPE-SMBs.</p>
         
         <p>Two types of 3D network polymers are used to construct GPE (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). The first one is poly(bis(3-(2-methylaziridin-1-yl) propanoate) (PBMP), and the second one is poly (trimethylolpropane triacrylate) (PTPA). PTPA was obtained by in situ radical initiated polymerization in the presence of 2,2′-azobis(2-methylpropionitrile) (AIBN).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0016_R_d250121866e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> PBMP was obtained by in situ cation-initiated polymerization in the presence of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, in which phosphorus pentafluoride was generated by the heat decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> as an initiator for polymerization (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a>, Supporting Information). The photos of the precursor solution before and after polymerization are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c,d</a> (Supporting Information). The successful synthesis of PTPA and PBMP is confirmed by Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e</a>, Supporting Information). PBMP-based GPE is prepared by polymerizing BMP in the fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (5:95 by volume, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>&nbsp;NaPF<sub>6</sub>) based electrolyte, and the thickness of PBMP-based GPE containing glassy fiber membrane is ≈ 25&nbsp;µm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1f</a>, Supporting Information). Liquid electrolyte (LE) composed of FEC and PC (5:95 by volume, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>&nbsp;NaPF<sub>6</sub>) was also used as reference electrolyte. Moreover, LE with 0.1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> tetraethylammonium nitrate (TEA) was also used as reference electrolyte to prepare Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> contained SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0030_R_d250121866e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4fb56d5e-85d3-4d3f-bbd2-033229502e5f/aenm202303791-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4fb56d5e-85d3-4d3f-bbd2-033229502e5f/aenm202303791-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/150ca0f4-7927-4c66-b5c0-99690bc8334b/aenm202303791-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4fb56d5e-85d3-4d3f-bbd2-033229502e5f/aenm202303791-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a)&nbsp;Schematic illustration of Na metal anode with PBMP-based SEI. b)The molecular structure from solvent, TPA, and BMP electrolyte. c) Binding energies comparison of Na-BMP, Na-TPA, Na-FEC, and corresponding structures. d) Charge density difference and electron donations comparison of Na-BMP, Na-TPA, and Na-FEC. e) LUMO energy comparison of BMP, TPA, and FEC molecules.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the chemical reactions between the components of electrolyte and Na metal, the density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out to identify the interaction. Compared with the binding energies of Na-PC (−0.31&nbsp;eV), Na-FEC (−0.25&nbsp;eV), and Na-TPA (−0.37&nbsp;eV), Na-BMP has a lower value (−1.02&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). It suggests BMP is preferentially reduced. Bader analysis is intuitive to verify this point again. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0001">1d</a>, the electron donation from Na to BMP is 0.91&nbsp;e, which is higher than those for Na to TEA (0.868&nbsp;e), Na to TPA (0.899&nbsp;e), Na to FEC (0.90&nbsp;e), and Na to PC (0.889&nbsp;e, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). It means that BMP has the priority to react with Na metal. It validates that the low-valence N element of BMP easily obtain electron from Na metal. We further calculated the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) of BMP, TEA, TPA, FEC, and PC. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), PBMP has a lower LUMO (0.19&nbsp;eV) compared with other chemicals (TPA: 0.91&nbsp;eV, PC: 1.0&nbsp;eV, FEC: 0.52&nbsp;eV). The results are consistent with above DFT analysis Compared with those of LE and GPE(PTPA), GPE(PBMP) with tertiary amine group is priority reacts with Na metal, which is possible to produce a high ionic conductivity and high mechanical strength SEI.</p>
         
         <p>To confirm the superiority of GPE(PBMP) for the cyclic stability of Na metal, we assembled various types batteries to confirm it (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The Na//Na cells with GPE(PBMP) can cycle stably more than 1100&nbsp;h at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, much longer than LE (240&nbsp;h) and GPE(PTPA) (550&nbsp;h, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0002">2a</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of assembled cells shows that Na//Na battery with GPE(PBMP) has the lower charge transfer resistance (900&nbsp;Ω) after cycles than those with LE (3420&nbsp;Ω) and GPE(PTPA) (2020&nbsp;Ω, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0002">2b</a>). As the cycle number increases, the resistance quickly increases due to the increased thickness of SEI, ascribing to the fast consumption of GPE(PTPA). In contrast, there is a little change for GPE(PBMP) after 100 cycles, indicating the less consumption of electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The result indicates more stable SEI produced on Na anode from GPE(PBMP) than those from LE and GPE(PTPA). Moreover, the GPE(PBMP) based Na//Na symmetrical batteries can operate stably even at the current densities of 0.5 and 1.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> with area capacity of 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), overcoming the failure operation of GPE(PTPA) and LE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0002">2d</a>). Notably, the cycle life ranks at the top-level among the reported data at the similar current density and capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the Na//Cu cells with GPE(PBMP) were assembled to check the Coulombic efficiency (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), and a CE of ≈93% is achieved. To evaluate the mechanical strength of two GPEs, we primarily tested the values by a universal material testing machine. The mechanical strength increases to ≈900&nbsp;kPa, when the volume compression is 30% of the original state, and the value of GPE(PBMP) is close to that of GPE(PTPA).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0031_R_d250121866e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The result indicates two GPEs have similar ability to inhibit dendrite growth (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). We measured the ionic conductivity by testing the resistance of stainless-steel//stainless-steel symmetrical cells. GPE(PBMP) has an ionic conductivity of ≈1.8&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, higher than that of GPE(PTPA) (≈0.62&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). To demonstrate the electrochemical stability of GPEs, we assembled the Na//stainless-steel batteries for the cyclic voltammetry (CV) test (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with LE, GPEs have an anti-oxidation potential up to 5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0482475e-aa2d-4ec2-a2c4-b09114bb0405/aenm202303791-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0482475e-aa2d-4ec2-a2c4-b09114bb0405/aenm202303791-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a0cf2ce8-4af3-4d09-a9b0-4d4016c13d6f/aenm202303791-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0482475e-aa2d-4ec2-a2c4-b09114bb0405/aenm202303791-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycle stability test of Na//Na symmetrical batteries at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 0.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) Electron impedance spectroscopy (EIS) test of Na//Na symmetrical batteries after 10 cycles. c) Cycle stability test of Na//Na symmetrical batteries at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) Polarization curves of Na//Na batteries at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The e) rate test and f) histogram polarization voltage of Na//Na symmetrical batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate GPE(PBMP) for rate capability, we tested Na//Na symmetrical batteries at different current density. Na/GPE(PBMP)/Na can cycle stably at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> with a low polarization voltage of 22, 28, 42, 56, 77, 147 and 140&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0002">2e,f</a>). In contrast, the increase of polarization voltage with LE and GPE(PTPA) indicates the lower utilization of inhomogeneous Na, poor electrochemical reversibility of Na plating/stripping and unstable SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0027_R_d250121866e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Joule">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It leads to the short-circuit of batteries at a current density of 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The key parameter for advanced SEI structure is the chemical species for the long-term cycle stability of Na metal. To check it more carefully, we used tetramethylammonium nitrate (TEA) as electrolyte additive to improve the Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> in SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0030_R_d250121866e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Our previous research indicated that TEA can be an advanced additive to improve the content of Li<sub>3</sub>N and LiN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> in SEI layer, resulting in the substantial improved cyclic stability of Li metal. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0003">3a</a> compared the cyclic stability of TEA supported LE, which is denoted as LE(TEA). Compared with PBMP-based Na//Na symmetrical cells, LE(TEA) based cell shows much lower cyclic stability. Furthermore, we check the chemical composition of SEI by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) with etching technique. XPS N 1s peaks at the binding energy of 398.2 and 399.3&nbsp;eV can be assigned to Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub>, which can be detected for both LE(TEA) and GPE(PBMP) derived SEI (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0031_R_d250121866e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As known, NaF has a high Young's modulus, which has the positive effect on the deposited morphology and cyclic stability of Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0032_R_d250121866e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0033_R_d250121866e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Small">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), more NaF and less oxidic organic can be detected in the GPE(PBMP) derived SEI than that from LE(TEA). However, the contents of the N element show a huge difference. Compared with the content of N in LE(TEA) based SEI (≈0.3&nbsp;mol%), GPE(PBMP) based SEI shows ≈1.5&nbsp;mol% content of N (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The highly conducive and concentrated Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> species in SEI provide a fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport route to inhibit the dendrite growth and side reactions, guaranteeing the long-term cyclic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0034_R_d250121866e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Environ. Sci.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0035_R_d250121866e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To visually analyze the chemical compositions and their spatial structure of SEI after cycling, their 3D tomography was investigated by time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0003">3c–e</a>). C<sup>−</sup> and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> are observed in the LE, LE(TEA), and GPE(PBMP) derived SEI (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). A thin layer with rich N, C<sup>−</sup> and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> species is observed in the GPE(PBMP) based SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0003">3c</a>), while a thick and inhomogeneous layer of N, C<sup>−</sup> and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> species in the LE(TEA) based SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0003">3d</a>). Compared with the thick LE(TEA) derived SEI layer with N-based species, only a thin SEI layer with rich N is detected within GPE(PBMP). It indicates that tertiary amine groups of GPE(PBMP) effectively generate ion conductive species in the SEI layer, which can benefit for uniform Na stripping and plating behaviors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0035_R_d250121866e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0037_R_d250121866e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d756fa0-fda7-4c79-8c63-1e3798fbc73c/aenm202303791-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d756fa0-fda7-4c79-8c63-1e3798fbc73c/aenm202303791-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cfa1dcf2-ae36-48ac-8b34-fa198a214c3d/aenm202303791-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d756fa0-fda7-4c79-8c63-1e3798fbc73c/aenm202303791-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The mechanism analysis of SEI layer decomposition. a) Cycle stability test of Na//Na symmetrical batteries at 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) depth etching element analysis of SEI layers with LE(TEA) and GPE(PBMP) on Na electrodes. 3D tomography of Na metal surface after cycling with c) LE(TEA) and d) GPE(PBMP) derived from TOF-SIMS. e) TOF-SIMS sputtering profiles of corresponding segments after cycling.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To confirm the morphology of SEI, the surface of Na metal was investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4a</a>, the surface of Na metal with GPE(PBMP) after 10 cycles is flat and smooth, while the surface with LE derived SEI is loose and porous (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4c</a>). The surface element mapping images were also shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). The content of N and F elements is relatively high, indicating that more inorganic materials are produced on the surface. The cross-section images show that the thickness of SEI layer of Na electrode with PBMP is only 8&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4b</a>), much thinner than that with LE (110&nbsp;µm) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4d</a>). The corresponding cross-section images of Na elemental mapping images was also shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4e,g</a>. A thin and dense SEI layer is benefited from Na<sup>+</sup> depositing and stripping. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> deposition behavior was monitored by using COMSOL Multiphysics, and the transient electrochemical deposition process on the surface of sodium metal electrodes was calculated using the cubic current distribution (Tertiary Current Distribution) module and the phase field module in a simplified 2D model. Under the electrochemical reduction process (Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> = Na), the current characteristics follow the Bulter–Volmer equation and Nernst equation. The result show that the concentration distributes and diffusion flux of electron between Na and GPE(PBMP) presents the good homogeneity from initial to steady state (0–7200&nbsp;s, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4f,h</a>). In contrast, Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;and electron with LE locally concentrate, resulting in an extremely nonuniform distribution after 7200&nbsp;s (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). It verifies that Na metal with GPE(PBMP) can uniform plating/stripping to keep an ultra-high stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4de11172-9863-403f-99bd-133d259cf4b0/aenm202303791-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4de11172-9863-403f-99bd-133d259cf4b0/aenm202303791-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/474c7730-1a74-4620-bfcf-4c10ce104952/aenm202303791-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4de11172-9863-403f-99bd-133d259cf4b0/aenm202303791-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Surface morphology characterization of Na electrodes after Na//Na symmetric cell cycled. SEM images of surface, cross-section, and corresponding element distributed with a,b,e) GPE(PBMP) and c,d,g) LE of Na electrodes after cycled. Distribution of Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;on the Na surfaces with f) GPE(PBMP) and h) LE at steady state via COMSOL Multiphysics simulation. In situ optical microscopy observation of Na metal electrochemical deposition with i,j) GPE(PBMP) and k,l) LE. m,n) In situ DEMS result of mass signals m/z 2 (H<sub>2</sub>) and 44 (CO<sub>2</sub>) with LE and GPE(PBMP). The distribution of Young's modulus on the surface of o) GPE(PBMP)-SEI and p) LE-SEI. (The scale bar is 5&nbsp;µm for a,c, 200&nbsp;µm for b,e,d,g and i–l).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To check the protection process of SEI on the cyclic stability of Na electrode, we assembled Na//Na cells and observed the Na deposition behavior by using in situ optical microscopy (OM) with a current density of 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4i,j</a>, the surface of Na metal with PBMP keeps smooth and flat during deposition process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0037_R_d250121866e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, a large number of dendrites and resultant gases quickly appear on the surface of Na metal with LE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4k,l</a>). To clarify gas products, we used in situ differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4m,n</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). A high quantity of hydrogen (H<sub>2</sub>) and carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) are detected in the LE-based cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0038_R_d250121866e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By comparison, almost no gas is detected in GPE(PBMP) based cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0039_R_d250121866e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Young's modulus distribution of SEI is quantitatively evaluated by atomic force microscope (AFM). GPE(PBMP) based SEI shows an average value of 6.3&nbsp;GPa, higher than that of LE-based SEI (3.4&nbsp;GPa) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0004">4o,p</a>). It might be due to that more inorganic species are generated from GPE(PBMP), and the result also proves that the SEI from GPE(PBMP) has good mechanical strength to inhibit dendrite growth and side reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0040_R_d250121866e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0042_R_d250121866e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Combining with OM observation, DEMS, and AFM analysis, we propose the possible reaction decomposition pathway of carbonate solvents (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0006">1</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0015_R_d250121866e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> FEC is one of the most effective co-solvents to construct SEI for better cycling of metal electrode, due to its possibility to polymerize through both the carbonate group via surface nucleophilic attacks and the double bond formed via HF elimination by surface basic species (Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0006">1</a>, reactions 1–3). Possible chemical reactions form the reactive cation radical FEC<sup>•+</sup>, which may attack the carbonyl carbon of FEC to form aldehyde and oligomers of alkyl carbonates, are shown in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0006">1</a>, reactions 4–7. Advantageous NaF and disadvantageous H<sub>2</sub>, CO<sub>2</sub> can be formed during cell cycling with LE. In the PBMP based GPE, NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>N primarily form, and a small amount of organic species form, and no highly negative effect exists (Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0006">1</a>, reactions 8–10). The nitride SEI can facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and improve the kinetics of the Na plating process, as indicated in the discussion of XPS results. Then the cell with GPE(PBMP) shows the long-term cyclic stability without limited dendrite growth and side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0043_R_d250121866e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Small Methods">43</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0045_R_d250121866e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Energy Chem.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b8762369-a620-4400-9c8e-cd023701616d/aenm202303791-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b8762369-a620-4400-9c8e-cd023701616d/aenm202303791-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85c1b1bd-1b0a-4ff2-94a6-5709181a1268/aenm202303791-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b8762369-a620-4400-9c8e-cd023701616d/aenm202303791-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Possible reactions of reduction of solvent on Na electrodes. Chemical reactions 1–7 correspond to the electrolyte is LE, and chemical reactions 8–10 correspond to the electrolyte is GPE(PBMP).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further evaluate the practical use of GPE(PBMP), the full batteries are assembled with sodium vanadium phosphate (NVP) as cathode material (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0005">5a–c</a>). At the rate of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 15 and 20&nbsp;C, the specific discharge capacities are 105.2, 102.3, 100.9, 98.6, 95.1, 89.7, and 83.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, higher than that of Na/LE/NVP (101.2, 96.3, 95.9, 93.1, 87.4, 80.1 and 74.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). It proves the formation of advanced SEI layer in the presence of GPE(PBMP) for the high ionic conductivity and excellent rate performance of the Na electrode. The GPE-SMBs with GPE(PBMP) show the cell capacity retention rate of as high as 91.7% at the current rate of 5&nbsp;C after 6000 cycles, much better than Na/LE/NVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0005">5d</a>). Na/GPE(PBMP)/ NVP cell exhibits a highly capacity retention ratio reaches to 97.8% at a current density of 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> after 2000 cycles. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0005">5e</a>). The discharge-charge curve in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303791-fig-0005">5f,g</a> shows that the specific capacity of GPE(PBMP) and LE after 2023 cycles are 91 and 8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which proves the SEI stability of GPE(PBMP). Such a high cyclic stability of Na metal electrode and GPE-SMB have highly competitive to the ever-reported data in the literatures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0015_R_d250121866e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0029_R_d250121866e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0043_R_d250121866e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Small Methods">43</a>-<a href="#aenm202303791-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303791-bib-0049_R_d250121866e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Eng. J.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We increase the loading of the cathode, it also can keep a capacity retention rate of 98.1% after 300 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). We also tested the flame retardancy condition by using the combustion experiment (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). It shows that the diaphragm with LE burns rapidly, but GPE(PBMP) is difficult to ignite, showing a good flame-retardant capability. The 3D network of PBMP has a good locking ability to liquid medium due to their strong interaction with high contact area, which can inhibit the liquid volatilization. Consequently, GPE(PBMP) becomes less flammable, and battery safety is improved.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303791-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e68b2ee6-bf9b-4e27-acc1-84690157182a/aenm202303791-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e68b2ee6-bf9b-4e27-acc1-84690157182a/aenm202303791-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8d70a4f-cb05-4a4f-a99a-a0b6d4d5872d/aenm202303791-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e68b2ee6-bf9b-4e27-acc1-84690157182a/aenm202303791-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303791-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303791" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The battery performance of SMBs. Charging and discharging curves of Na//NVP full batteries with a) GPE(PBMP) and b) LE at 1, 5, and 20&nbsp;C. c) Histogram of special capacity corresponding Na//NVP full batteries with LE and GPE(PBMP) at different rates. d,f) Cyclic stability of Na//NVP full battery with LE and GPE(PBMP) at 0.5 and 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. e,g) Charging and discharging curves of Na//NVP full batteries with GPE(PBMP) and LE&nbsp;at different cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303791-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303791-sec-0020-title">2 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, different from the ever-reported in situ SEI from electrolyte additive or polymer, we prepare tertiary-amine group-based network polymer to construct an ion conductive and high mechanical strength SEI for GPE-SMBs. Thanks for the low valance state of N in PBMP polymer, a preferable chemical reaction between N and Na metal generates the thin and rich Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> species in SEI, resulting in the extended cycle life of Na metal. As a result, the capacity retention rate of NVP-based GPE-SMBs reaches as high as 97.8% after 2000 cycles at current density of 5&nbsp;C (2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). This work provides a new strategy for constructing an advanced SEI from the viewpoint of network polymer design for stable GPE-SMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
